<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>*<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> joins all but Part III-B-1 of this opinion.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As originally enacted, 2 provided that "[n]o voting qualification or prerequisite to voting, or standard, practice, or procedure shall be imposed or applied by any State or political subdivision to deny or abridge the right of any citizen of the United States to vote on account of race or color."  42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1973 (1970 ed.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Judge Manasco, presiding in <em>Caster</em>, also preliminarily enjoined Alabama from using HB1.  Her opinion was based on the same evidentiary record as was before the three-judge Court, and it adopted in full that Court's "recitation of the evidence, legal analysis, findings of fact and conclusions of law."  1 App. to Emergency Application for Stay in No. 2:21-cv-1536, p.&nbsp;4; see also 582 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d, at 942-943, and n.&nbsp;4.  Any reference to the "District Court" in this opinion applies to the <em>Caster</em> Court as well as to the three-judge Court.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent complains that "what the District Court did here is essentially no different from what many courts have done for decades under this Court's superintendence."  <em>Post</em>, at 47 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).  That is not such a bad definition of <em>stare decisis</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Despite this all, the dissent argues that courts have apparently been "methodically carving the country into racially designated electoral districts" for decades.  <em>Post</em>, at 48 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).  And that, the dissent inveighs, "should inspire us to repentance."  <em>Ibid.</em>  But proportional representation of minority voters is absent from nearly every corner of this country despite 2 being in effect for over 40 years.  And in case after case, we have rejected districting plans that would bring States closer to proportionality when those plans violate traditional districting criteria.  See <em>supra</em>, at 19-21.  It seems it is the dissent that is "quixotically joust[ing] with an imaginary adversary."  <em>Post</em>, at 47 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent claims that Cooper "treated 'the minority population in and of itself&nbsp;' as the paramount community of interest in his plans."  <em>Post</em>, at 14 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (quoting 2 App. 601).  But Cooper testified that he was "aware that the minority population in and of itself <em>can</em> be a community of interest."  <em>Id.,</em> at 601 (emphasis added).  Cooper then explained that the relevant community of interest here--the Black Belt--was a "<em>historical</em> feature" of the State, not a demographic one.  <em>Ibid. </em>(emphasis added).  The Black Belt, he emphasized, was defined by its "historical boundaries"--namely, the group of "rural counties plus Montgomery County in the central part of the state."  <em>Ibid.</em>  The District Court treated the Black Belt as a community of interest for the same reason.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The dissent also protests that Cooper's "plans prioritized race over neutral districting criteria."  <em>Post</em>, at 14 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).  But as the District Court found, and as Alabama does not contest, Cooper's maps satisfied other traditional criteria, such as compactness, contiguity, equal populations, and respect for political subdivisions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Dr. Duchin created her two million map sample as part of an academic article that she helped author, not for her work on this case, and the article was neither entered into evidence below nor made part of the record here.  See 2 App. 710; see also M. Duchin &amp; D. Spencer, Models, Race, and the Law, 130 Yale L. J. Forum 744, 763-764 (2021) (Duchin &amp; Spencer).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The principal dissent decrees that Dr. Duchin's and Dr. Imai's maps are "surely probative," forgiving the former's use of stale census data as well as both mapmakers' collective failure to incorporate many traditional districting guidelines.  <em>Post</em>, at 23-24, and n.&nbsp;14 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.); see also <em>post</em>, at 15, n.&nbsp;9, 16.  In doing so, that dissent ignores Dr. Duchin's testimony that--when using the correct census data--the "randomized algorithms" she employed "found plans with two majority-black districts in literally thousands of different ways."  MSA 316-317.  The principal dissent and the dissent by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> also ignore Duchin's testimony that "it is certainly possible" to draw the illustrative maps she produced in a race-blind manner.  2 App. 713.  In that way, even the race-blind standard that the dissents urge would be satisfied here.  See <em>post</em>, at 21 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.); <em>post</em>, at 6 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J.).  So too could that standard be satisfied in every 2 case; after all, as Duchin explained, any map produced in a deliberately race-predominant manner would necessarily emerge at some point in a random, race-neutral process.  2 App. 713.  And although <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> voices support for an "old-school approach" to 2, even that approach cannot be squared with his understanding of <em>Gingles</em>.  <em>Post</em>, at 6.  The very reason a plaintiff adduces a map at the first step of <em>Gingles</em> is precisely <em>because</em> <em>of </em>its racial composition--that is, because it creates an additional majority-minority district that does not then exist.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;None of this is to suggest that algorithmic mapmaking is categorically irrelevant in voting rights cases.  Instead, we note only that, in light of the difficulties discussed above, courts should exercise caution before treating results produced by algorithms as all but dispositive of a 2 claim.  And in evaluating algorithmic evidence more generally in this context, courts should be attentive to the concerns we have discussed.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent suggests that <em>Growe</em> does not support the proposition that 2 applies to single-member redistricting.  <em>Post</em>, at 4-5 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).  The Court has understood <em>Growe</em> much differently.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Abrams </em>v. <em>Johnson</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 74, 90 (1997) ("Our decision in [<em>Gingles</em>] set out the basic framework for establishing a vote dilution claim against at-large, multimembers districts; we have since extended the framework to single-member districts." (citing <em>Growe</em>, 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 40-41)); <em>Johnson</em> v.<em> De Grandy</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S. 997, 1006 (1994) ("In <em>Growe</em>, we held that a claim of vote dilution in a single-member district requires proof meeting <em>the same </em>three threshold conditions for a dilution challenge to a multimember district .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;."); <em>Bartlett</em> v. <em>Strickland</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 12 (plurality opinion) ("The Court later held that the three <em>Gingles</em> requirements apply equally in 2 cases involving single-member districts .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;." (citing <em>Growe</em>, 507 U.&nbsp;S., at 40-41)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> argues that "[t]he <em>Gingles</em> framework should be [re]interpreted" in light of changing methods in statutory interpretation.  <em>Post</em>, at 10 (dissenting opinion).  But as we have explained, <em>Gingles</em> effectuates the delicate legislative bargain that 2 embodies.  And statutory <em>stare decisis</em> counsels strongly in favor of not "undo[ing] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the compromise that was reached between the House and Senate when 2 was amended in 1982."  <em>Brnovich</em>, 594 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 22).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Unlike ordinary statutory precedents, the "Court's precedents applying common-law statutes and pronouncing the Court's own interpretive methods and principles typically do not fall within that category of stringent statutory <em>stare decisis</em>."  <em>Ramos</em>,<em> </em>590 U.&nbsp;S., at ___, n.&nbsp;2 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J.) (slip op., at 5, n.&nbsp;2); see also, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Kisor</em> v. <em>Wilkie</em>, 588 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment) (slip op., at 34-36); <em>id.</em>, at ___-___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment) (slip op., at 1-2); <em>Leegin Creative Leather Products, Inc. </em>v. <em>PSKS, Inc.</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 877, 899-907 (2007); <em>Arbaugh</em> v. <em>Y &amp; H Corp.</em>, 546 U.&nbsp;S. 500, 510-516 (2006).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To ensure that <em>Gingles</em> does not improperly morph into a proportionality mandate, courts must rigorously apply the "geographically compact" and "reasonably configured" requirements.  See <em>ante</em>, at 22 (2 requirements under <em>Gingles</em> are "exacting").  In this case, for example, it is important that at least some of the plaintiffs' proposed alternative maps respect county lines at least as well as Alabama's redistricting plan.  See <em>ante</em>, at 12.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"No person acting under color of law shall .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. in determining whether any individual is qualified under State law or laws to vote in any election, apply any standard, practice, or procedure different from the standards, practices, or procedures applied under such law or laws to other individuals within the same county, parish, or similar political subdivision who have been found by State officials to be qualified to vote."  52 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 10101(a)(2)(A).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority suggests that districting lines are a "&nbsp;'prerequisite to voting'&nbsp;" because they "determin[e] where" voters "cast [their] ballot[s]."  <em>Ante</em>, at 32.  But, of course, a voter's polling place is a separate matter from the district to which he is assigned, and communities are often moved between districts without changing where their residents go to vote.  The majority's other example ("who [voters] are eligible to vote for," <em>ibid.</em>) is so far a stretch from the Act's focus on voting qualifications and voter action that it speaks for itself.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority chides Alabama for declining to specifically argue that 2 is inapplicable to multimember and at-large districting plans.  But these cases are about a single-member districting plan, and it is hardly uncommon for parties to limit their arguments to the question presented.  Further, while I do not myself believe that the text of 2 applies to multimember or at-large plans, the idea that such plans might be especially problematic from a vote-dilution standpoint is hardly foreign to the Court's precedents, see <em>Johnson</em> v. <em>De Grandy</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S. 997, 1012 (1994); <em>Growe</em> v. <em>Emison</em>, 507 U.&nbsp;S. 25, 40 (1993); cf. <em>Holder </em>v. <em>Hall</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S. 874, 888 (1994) (O'Connor, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment) (explaining that single-member districts may provide the benchmark when multimember or at-large systems are challenged, but suggesting no benchmark for challenges to single-member districts), or to the historical evolution of vote-dilution claims.  Neither the case from which the 1982 Congress drew 2(b)'s current operative language, see <em>White</em> v. <em>Regester</em>, 412 U.&nbsp;S. 755, 766 (1973), nor the one it was responding to, <em>Mobile</em> v. <em>Bolden</em>, 446 U.&nbsp;S. 55 (1980), involved single-member districts.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span>'s partial concurrence emphasizes the supposedly enhanced <em>stare decisis </em>force of statutory-interpretation precedents.  See <em>ante</em>, at 1-2.  This emphasis is puzzling in several respects.  As an initial matter, I can perceive no conceptual "basis for applying a heightened version of <em>stare decisis </em>to statutory-interpretation decisions"; rather, "our judicial duty is to apply the law to the facts of the case, regardless of how easy it is for the law to change."  <em>Gamble </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring) (slip op., at 14).  Nor does that approach appear to have any historical foundation in judicial practice at  the founding or for more than a century thereafter.  See T. Lee, Stare Decisis in Historical Perspective: From the Founding Era to the Rehnquist Court, 52 Vand. L.&nbsp;Rev. 647, 708-732 (1999).  But, even putting those problems aside, any appeal to heightened statutory <em>stare decisis </em>is particularly misplaced in this context.  As the remainder of this dissent explains in depth, the Court's 2 precedents differ from "ordinary statutory precedents" in two vital ways.  <em>Ante</em>, at 2, n.&nbsp;1 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J.).  The first is their profound tension with the Constitution's hostility to racial classifications, a tension that <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> acknowledges and that makes every 2 question the reverse side of a corresponding constitutional question.  See <em>ante</em>, at 4.<em>  </em>The second is that, to whatever extent 2 applies to districting, it can only "be understood as a delegation of authority to the courts to develop a common law of racially fair elections."  C. Elmendorf, Making Sense of Section 2: Of Biased Votes, Unconstitutional Elections, and Common Law Statutes, 160 U. Pa. L.&nbsp;Rev. 377, 383 (2012).  It would be absurd to maintain that this Court's "notoriously unclear and confusing" 2 case law follows, in any straightforward way, from the statutory text's high-flown language about the equal openness of political processes.  <em>Merrill</em> v. <em>Milligan</em>, 595 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2022) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J., concurring in grant of applications for stays) (slip op., at 6).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Like the majority, I refer to both courts below as "the District Court" without distinction.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;While <em>Brnovich</em> involved a time-place-and-manner voting rule, not a vote-dilution challenge to a districting plan, its analysis logically must apply to vote-dilution cases if the text of 2 covers such claims at all.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;District 7 owes its majority-black status to a 1992 court order.  See <em>Wesch</em> v. <em>Hunt</em>, 785 F.&nbsp;Supp. 1491, 1493-1494, 1496-1497, 1501-1502 (SD Ala.), aff&nbsp;'d <em>sub nom.</em> <em>Camp</em> v. <em>Wesch</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S. 902 (1992).  At the time, the Justice Department's approach to preclearance under 5 of the Act followed the "so-called 'max-black' policy," which "required States, including Alabama, to create supermajority-black voting districts or face denial of preclearance."  <em>Alabama Legislative Black Caucus</em> v. <em>Alabama</em>, 575 U.&nbsp;S. 254, 298 (2015) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., dissenting).  Although <em>Wesch</em> was a 2 case and the court-imposed plan that resulted was not subject to preclearance, see 785 F.&nbsp;Supp., at 1499-1500, there can be little doubt that a similar ethos dominated that litigation, in which all parties stipulated to the desirability of a <em>65%-plus</em> majority-black district.  See <em>id.</em>, at 1498-1499.  To satisfy that dubious need, the <em>Wesch </em>court aggressively adjusted the northeast and southeast corners of the previous District 7.  In the northeast, where District 7 once encompassed all of Tuscaloosa County and the more or less rectangular portion of Jefferson County not included in District 6, the 1992 plan drew a long, thin "finger" that traversed the southeastern third of Tuscaloosa County to reach deep into the  heart of urban Birmingham.  See Supp. App. 207-208.  Of the Jefferson County residents captured by the "finger," 75.48% were black.  <em>Wesch</em>, 785 F.&nbsp;Supp., at 1569.  In the southeast, District 7 swallowed a jigsaw-shaped portion of Montgomery County, the residents of which were 80.18% black.  <em>Id.</em>, at 1575.  Three years later, in <em>Miller</em> v. <em>Johnson</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S. 900, 923-927 (1995), we rejected the "max-black" policy as unwarranted by 5 and inconsistent with the Constitution.  But "much damage to the States' congressional and legislative district maps had already been done," including in Alabama.  <em>Alabama Legislative Black Caucus</em>, 575 U.&nbsp;S., at 299 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I have included an Appendix, <em>infra</em>, illustrating the plaintiffs' 11 proposed maps.  The first 10 images display the "black-only" voting-age population of census-designated voting districts in relation to the maps' hypothetical district lines.  The record does not contain a similar illustration for the 11th map, but a simple visual comparison with the other maps suffices.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority notes that this study used demographic data from the 2010 census, not the 2020 one.  That is irrelevant, since the black population share in Alabama changed little (from 26.8% to 27.16%) between the two censuses.  To think that this minor increase might have changed Dr. Duchin's results would be to entirely miss her point: that proportional representation for <em>any</em> minority, unless achieved "by design," is a statistical anomaly in almost all single-member-districting systems.  Duchin &amp; Spencer 764.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Of course, bizarreness is in the eye of the beholder, and, while labels like "&nbsp;'tentacles'&nbsp;" or "&nbsp;'appendages'&nbsp;" have no ultimate legal significance, it is far from clear that they do not apply here.  See <em>ante</em>, at 12.  The tendrils with which the various versions of illustrative District 2 would  capture black Mobilians are visually striking and are easily recognized as a racial grab against the backdrop of the State's demography.  The District 7 "finger," which encircles the black population of the Birmingham metropolitan area in order to separate them from their white neighbors and link them with black rural areas in the west of the State, also stands out to the naked eye.  The District Court disregarded the "finger" because it has been present in every districting plan since 1992, including the State's latest enacted plan.  <em>Singleton </em>v. <em>Merrill</em>, 582 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 924, 1011 (ND Ala. 2022) (<em>per&nbsp;curiam</em>).  But that reasoning would allow plaintiffs to bootstrap one racial gerrymander as a reason for permitting a second.  Because the question is not before us, I express no opinion on whether existing District 7 is constitutional as enacted by the State.  It is indisputable, however, that race predominated in the original creation of the district, see n.&nbsp;7, <em>supra</em>, and it is plain that the primary race-neutral justification for the district today must be the State's legitimate interest in "preserving the cores of prior districts" and the fact that the areas constituting District 7's core have been grouped together for decades.  <em>Karcher</em> v. <em>Daggett</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 725, 740 (1983); see also <em>id.</em>, at 758 (Stevens, J., concurring) (explaining that residents of a political unit "often develop a community of interest").  The plaintiffs' maps, however, necessarily would require the State to assign little weight to core retention with respect to other districts.  There could then be no principled race-neutral justification for prioritizing core retention only when it preserved an existing majority-black district, while discarding it when it stood in the way of creating a new one.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The equal-population baseline for Alabama's seven districts is 717,154 persons per district.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality's somewhat elliptical discussion of "the line between racial predominance and racial consciousness," <em>ante</em>, at 23, suggests that it may have fallen into a similar error.  To the extent the plurality supposes that, under our precedents, a State may purposefully sort voters based on race to some indefinite extent without crossing the line into predominance, it is wrong, and its predominance analysis would water down decades of racial-gerrymandering jurisprudence.  Our constitutional<em> </em>precedents' line between racial awareness and racial predominance simply tracks the distinction between awareness of consequences, on the one hand, and discriminatory <em>purpose</em>, on the other.  See <em>Miller</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 916 ("&nbsp;'Discriminatory purpose implies more than intent as volition or intent as awareness of consequences.  It implies that the decisionmaker selected or reaffirmed a particular course of action at least in part "because of," not merely "in spite of," its adverse effects'&nbsp;" (alterations and some internal quotation marks omitted)); accord, <em>Shaw I</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S. 630, 646 (1993).  And our statements that 2 "demands consideration of race," <em>Abbott</em> v. <em>Perez</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2018) (slip op., at 4), and uses a "race-conscious calculus," <em>De Grandy</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S., at 1020, did not imply that a State can ever purposefully sort voters on a race-predominant basis without triggering strict scrutiny.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The plurality's reasoning does not withstand scrutiny even on its own terms.  Like Dr. Duchin, Mr. Cooper found it "necessary to consider race" to construct two majority-black districts, 2 App. 591, and he frankly acknowledged "reconfigur[ing]" the southern part of the State "to create the second African-American majority district," <em>id.</em>, at 610.  Further, his conclusory statement that race did not "predominate" in his plans, <em>id.</em>, at 595, must be interpreted in light of the rest of his testimony and the record as a whole.  Mr. Cooper recognized communities of interest as a traditional districting principle, but he applied that principle in a nakedly race-focused manner, explaining that "the minority population in and of itself&nbsp;" was the community of interest that was "top of mind as [he] was drawing the plan[s]."  <em>Id.</em>, at 601.  As noted, he also testified that he considered "minority voting strengt[h]" to be a "traditional redistricting principl[e]" in its own right.  <em>Id.</em>, at 591.  His testimony therefore buttresses, rather than undermines, the conclusion already obvious from the maps themselves: Only a mapmaker pursuing a fixed racial target would produce them.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority points to limitations of Dr. Duchin's and Dr. Imai's algorithms that do not undermine the strong inference from their results to the conclusion that no two-majority-black-district plan could be an appropriate proxy for the undiluted benchmark.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 26, 28-29. <em> </em>I have already explained why the fact that Dr. Duchin's study used 2010 census data is irrelevant.  See n.&nbsp;9, <em>supra</em>.  As for the algorithms' inability to incorporate all possible districting considerations, the absence of additional <em>constraints </em>cannot explain their failure to produce any maps hitting the plaintiffs' preferred racial target.  Next, while it is true that the number of possible districting plans is extremely large, that does not mean it is impossible to generate a statistically significant sample.  Here, for instance, Dr. Imai explained that "10,000 simulated plans" was sufficient to "yield statistically precise conclusions" and that any higher number would "not materially affect" the results.  Supp. App. 60.  Finally, the majority notes Dr. Duchin's testimony that her "exploratory algorithms" found "thousands" of possible two-majority-black-district maps.  2 App. 622; see <em>ante</em>, at 27, n.&nbsp;7.  Setting aside that Dr. Duchin never provided the denominator of which those "thousands" were the numerator, it is no wonder that the algorithms in question generated such maps; as Dr.  Duchin explained, she programmed them with "an algorithmic preference" for "plans in which there would be a second majority-minority district."  2 App. 709.  Thus, all that those algorithmic results prove is that it is <em>possible</em> to draw two majority-black districts in Alabama if one sets out to do so, especially with the help of sophisticated mapmaking software.  What is still lacking is any justification for treating a two-majority-black-district map as a proxy for the undiluted benchmark.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority lodges a similar accusation against the State's arguments (or what it takes to be the State's arguments).  See <em>ante</em>, at 18 ("Alabama suggests there is only one 'circumstance' that matters--how the State's map stacks up relative to the benchmark" (alteration omitted)).  But its rebuke is misplaced.  The "totality of circumstances" means that courts must consider all circumstances relevant to an issue.  It does not mean that they are forbidden to attempt to define the substantive standard that governs that issue.  In arguing that a vote-dilution claim requires judging a State's plan relative to an undiluted benchmark to be drawn from the totality of circumstances--including, where probative, the results of districting simulations--the State argues little more than what we have long acknowledged.  See <em>Reno </em>v.<em> Bossier Parish School Bd.</em>,<em> </em>520 U.&nbsp;S. 471, 480 (1997).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To the extent it is any sort of answer to the benchmark question, it tends inevitably toward proportionality.  By equating a voting minority's inability to win elections with a vote that has been "render[ed] .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. unequal," <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 17, the majority assumes "that members of [a] minority are denied a fully effective use of the franchise unless they are able to control seats in an elected body."  <em>Holder</em>, 512 U.&nbsp;S., at 899 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.).  That is precisely the assumption that leads to the proportional-control benchmark.  See <em>id.</em>, at 902, 937.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indeed, the majority's attempt to deflect this analysis only confirms its accuracy.  The majority stresses that its understanding of <em>Gingles </em>permits the rejection of "plans that would bring States closer to proportionality <em>when those plans violate traditional districting criteria</em>."  <em>Ante</em>, at 21, n.&nbsp;4 (emphasis added).  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span>, similarly, defends <em>Gingles </em>against the charge of "mandat[ing] a proportional number of  majority-minority districts" by emphasizing that it requires only the creation of majority-minority districts that are compact and reasonably configured.  <em>Ante</em>, at 2 (opinion concurring in part).  All of this precisely tracks my point: As construed by the District Court and the majority, 2 mandates an ever closer approach to proportional control that stops only when a court decides that a further step in that direction would no longer be consistent with any reasonable application of traditional districting criteria.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Bethune-Hill </em>v. <em>Virginia State Bd. of Elections</em>, 580 U.&nbsp;S. 178 (2017), the Court upheld a race-predominant district based on the assumed compelling interest of complying with 5 of the Voting Rights Act.  <em>Id.</em>, at 193-196.  There, the Court was explicit that it was still merely "assum[ing], without deciding," that the asserted interest was compelling, as the plaintiffs "d[id] not dispute that compliance with 5 was a compelling interest at the relevant time."  <em>Id.</em>, at 193.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;While our congruence-and-proportionality cases have focused primarily on the Fourteenth Amendment, they make clear that the same principles govern "Congress' parallel power to enforce the provisions of the Fifteenth Amendment."  <em>City of Boerne</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S., at 518.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This formulation does not specifically account for the District Court's findings under the Senate factors, which, as I have explained, lack any traceable logical connection to the finding of a districting wrong or the need for a districting remedy.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span>, at least, recognizes that 2's constitutional footing is problematic, for he agrees that "race-based redistricting cannot extend indefinitely into the future."  <em>Ante</em>, at 4 (opinion concurring in part).  Nonetheless, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> votes to sustain a system of institutionalized racial discrimination in districting--under the aegis of a statute that applies nationwide and has no expiration date--and thus to prolong the "lasting harm to our society" caused by the use of racial classifications in the allocation of political power.  <em>Shaw I</em>, 509 U.&nbsp;S., at 657.  I cannot agree with that approach.  The Constitution no more tolerates this discrimination today than it will tolerate it tomorrow.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court does not address whether 2 contains a private right of action, an issue that was argued below but was not raised in this Court.  See <em>Brnovich</em> v. <em>Democratic National Committee</em>, 594 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2021) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J., concurring) (slip op., at 1).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Alabama's districting guidelines explicitly incorporate this nonpredominance requirement.  See <em>Singleton</em> v. <em>Merrill</em>, 582 F.&nbsp;Supp. 3d 924, 1036 (ND Ala. 2022<em>).</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although our cases have posited that racial predominance may be acceptable if strict scrutiny is satisfied, the Court does not contend that it is satisfied here.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The second and third <em>Gingles </em>preconditions, which concern racially polarized voting, cannot contribute to avoiding a clash between 2 and the Constitution over racial predominance in the drawing of lines.  Those preconditions do not concern the drawing of lines in plaintiffs' maps, and in any event, because voting in much of the South is racially polarized, they are almost always satisfied anyway.  Alabama does not contest that they are satisfied here.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court appears to contend that it does not matter if race predominated in the drawing of these maps because the maps <em>could have </em>been drawn without race predominating.  See <em>ante</em>, at 26-27, n.&nbsp;7.  But of course, many policies <em>could be </em>selected for race-neutral reasons.  They nonetheless must be assessed under the relevant standard for intentional reliance on race if their imposition was in fact motivated by race.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Hunter</em> v. <em>Underwood</em>, 471 U.&nbsp;S. 222, 227-231 (1985); <em>Arlington Heights</em> v. <em>Metropolitan Housing Development Corp.</em>, 429 U.&nbsp;S. 252, 264-266 (1977); <em>Washington</em> v. <em>Davis</em>, 426 U.&nbsp;S. 229, 241-248 (1976).</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

  }
  document.cookie = cookieName+"="+cookieValue+"; expires="+expireDays+'; path='+path+'; domain='+_satellite.getVar('host_root');
};

          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      }
    }

    //data-search-location
    var linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    for (var i = 0; i < linksWithSearchLocation.length; i++) {
      linksWithSearchLocation[i].addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = $(this).data("search-location") || $(this).parents("[data-search-location]").data("search-location");
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    }

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
});</script><script>
function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

setTrackerCookie('fl_last_page_view_id', _satellite.getVar('page_view_id') );
